      "source": [
        "# Melt the DataFrame to convert year columns (F1961-F2022) into 'Year' and 'Value' columns\n",
        "year_cols = [col for col in climate_change_df.columns if col.startswith('F') and col[1:].isdigit()]\n",
        "id_vars = [col for col in climate_change_df.columns if col not in year_cols]\n",
        "\n",
        "climate_change_long_df = climate_change_df.melt(id_vars=id_vars,\n",
        "                                           value_vars=year_cols,\n",
        "                                           var_name='Year',\n",
        "                                           value_name='Value')\n",
        "\n",
        "# Convert 'Year' column from 'FXXXX' format to integer year\n",
        "climate_change_long_df['Year'] = climate_change_long_df['Year'].str[1:].astype(int)\n",
        "\n",
        "# Handle missing values in the 'Value' column (impute with median)\n",
        "median_value_climate = climate_change_long_df['Value'].median()\n",
        "climate_change_long_df['Value'].fillna(median_value_climate, inplace=True)\n",
        "\n",
        "# Drop rows with missing 'ISO2' if any, as it's a country identifier\n",
        "climate_change_long_df.dropna(subset=['ISO2'], inplace=True)\n",
        "\n",
        "print(\"\\n--- Climate Change Indicators Dataset after preprocessing (long format) ---\")\n",
        "display(climate_change_long_df.head())\n",
        "print(climate_change_long_df.info())"
      ],

        "# Pivot the climate_change_long_df to have indicators as columns\n",
        "# We'll use Country, Year, ISO3 as identifiers and Indicator as the column to pivot\n",
        "# Using 'first' for aggregation in case of duplicate Country-Year-Indicator combinations, though ideally there shouldn't be.\n",
        "climate_change_indicators_pivoted_df = climate_change_long_df.pivot_table(\n",
        "    index=['Country', 'Year', 'ISO3'],\n",
        "    columns='Indicator',\n",
        "    values='Value',\n",
        "    aggfunc='first'\n",
        ").reset_index()\n",
        "\n",
        "# Rename the pivoted columns for better readability (remove special characters if any)\n",
        "climate_change_indicators_pivoted_df.columns.name = None # Remove the 'Indicator' name from columns\n",
        "\n",
        "print(\"\\n--- Climate Change Indicators Pivoted Dataset ---\")\n",

        "### Merging the Datasets\n",
        "\n",
        "Now, I will merge the processed `renewable_energy_df` with the `climate_change_indicators_pivoted_df` on 'Country' and 'Year' to create a comprehensive dataset for our analysis."
      ]

    {
      "cell_type": "code",

      "source": [
        "# Merge the two datasets on 'Country' and 'Year'\n",
        "# Using an inner merge to keep only the common countries and years in both datasets\n",
        "merged_df = pd.merge(renewable_energy_df,\n",
        "                     climate_change_indicators_pivoted_df,\n",
        "                     on=['Country', 'Year'],\n",
        "                     how='inner')\n",
        "\n",
        "print(\"\\n--- Merged Dataset ---\")\n",
        "display(merged_df.head())\n",

      "source": [
        "## 3. Generalizations, Analysis, and Understanding of the Dataset\n",
        "\n",
        "Now that we have merged the two datasets, let's analyze the `merged_df` to understand its structure, content, and the relationships between its features.\n",
        "\n",
        "### Generalizations and Observations:\n",
        "\n",
        "*   **Combined Information**: The `merged_df` now contains information about renewable energy consumption and various climate change indicators for different countries and years.\n",
        "*   **Key Features**: We have 'Country' and 'Year' as primary identifiers. `Value` from `renewable_energy_df` now represents a specific energy metric, and we have multiple new columns representing different climate indicators (e.g., 'Temperature change...', 'GHG emissions...').\n",
        "*   **Potential Relationships**: We can now investigate potential relationships, such as:\n",
        "    *   How does the `renewable_share_of_electricity` relate to `Temperature change`?\n",
        "    *   Are countries with higher `energy_consumption` also having higher `GHG emissions`?\n",
        "    *   What are the trends of these indicators over `Year` for different `Country`s?\n",
        "*   **Data Granularity**: The data is aggregated at a country-year level, which is suitable for macro-level analysis.\n",
        "\n",
        "### Handling Remaining Null Values\n",
        "\n",
        "Although we've handled nulls in individual dataframes, merging might introduce new `NaN`s if a country-year combination exists in one dataset but not the other, or if certain indicators were not present for all country-year combinations even after pivoting. Let's check for any remaining null values in the `merged_df` and decide on a strategy."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "code",
      "metadata": {
        "colab": {
          "base_uri": "https://localhost:8080/"
        },
        "id": "e842fcb9",
        "outputId": "f7fb76a1-f994-4f5d-dbe5-85254a03088a"
      },
      "source": [
        "# Check for remaining null values in the merged dataset\n",
        "print(\"\\n--- Missing values in Merged Dataset ---\")\n",
        "print(merged_df.isnull().sum()[merged_df.isnull().sum() > 0])\n",
        "\n",
        "# For simplicity, we'll fill any remaining numerical NaN values with the median of their respective columns.\n",
        "# This is a common strategy when the proportion of missing values is not too high and we want to retain data.\n",
        "for col in merged_df.select_dtypes(include=np.number).columns:\n",
        "    if merged_df[col].isnull().any():\n",
        "        median_val = merged_df[col].median()\n",
        "        merged_df[col].fillna(median_val, inplace=True)\n",
        "\n",
        "print(\"\\n--- Missing values after final imputation ---\")\n",
        "print(merged_df.isnull().sum()[merged_df.isnull().sum() > 0])"
      ],
      "execution_count": null,
      "outputs": [
        {
          "output_type": "stream",
          "name": "stdout",
          "text": [
            "\n",
            "--- Missing values in Merged Dataset ---\n",
            "Series([], dtype: int64)\n",
            "\n",
            "--- Missing values after final imputation ---\n",
            "Series([], dtype: int64)\n"
          ]
        }
      ]

      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "0827a4b9"
      },
      "source": [
        "### Feature Engineering (Column Selection)\n",
        "\n",
        "The `INDICATOR`, `SUBJECT`, `MEASURE`, and `FREQUENCY` columns from the original `renewable_energy_df` describe the `Value` column itself (total renewable energy in KTOE, annually). Since we have specific climate change indicators from the other dataset, these columns from the renewable energy data can be dropped to simplify our feature set, as the `Value` column directly represents the quantity we're interested in.\n",
        "\n",
        "Also, `ObjectId` is just an identifier and can be dropped."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "code",
      "metadata": {
        "colab": {
          "base_uri": "https://localhost:8080/",
          "height": 434
        },
        "id": "6347f054",
        "outputId": "ad7752b2-3521-4c7e-96d2-d4f70d7ca711"
      },
      "source": [
        "columns_to_drop = ['INDICATOR', 'SUBJECT', 'MEASURE', 'FREQUENCY', 'ObjectId', 'CTS_Code', 'CTS_Name', 'CTS_Full_Descriptor', 'Source', 'Unit']\n",
        "# Drop columns that are metadata or not relevant for direct modeling\n",
        "merged_df.drop(columns=columns_to_drop, inplace=True, errors='ignore')\n",
        "\n",
        "print(\"\\n--- Merged Dataset after Feature Engineering ---\")\n",
        "display(merged_df.head())\n",
        "print(merged_df.info())"

      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "e961a13b"
      },
      "source": [
        "## Fixing the Merge Issue: Harmonizing Country Identifiers\n",
        "\n",
        "The previous investigation revealed that the 'Country' column in `renewable_energy_df` contains ISO codes (e.g., 'AUS'), while the 'Country' column in `climate_change_indicators_pivoted_df` uses full country names. To resolve this, we will use the 'ISO3' column from `climate_change_indicators_pivoted_df` as our common country identifier, renaming it to 'Country' for consistency before merging."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "code",
      "metadata": {
        "colab": {
          "base_uri": "https://localhost:8080/",
          "height": 677
        },
        "id": "51f70762",
        "outputId": "59d4fb35-58c9-4bb1-a3ac-dd2d67f4e477"
      },
      "source": [
        "# Rename 'ISO3' to 'Country' in climate_change_indicators_pivoted_df to match renewable_energy_df's country identifier format\n",
        "climate_change_indicators_pivoted_df.rename(columns={'ISO3': 'Country_ISO'}, inplace=True)\n",
        "\n",
        "# Now, let's merge on the common ISO country code and Year\n",
        "# We'll use 'Country_ISO' from climate_change_indicators_pivoted_df and 'Country' from renewable_energy_df\n",
        "merged_df = pd.merge(renewable_energy_df,\n",
        "                     climate_change_indicators_pivoted_df,\n",
        "                     left_on=['Country', 'Year'],\n",
        "                     right_on=['Country_ISO', 'Year'],\n",
        "                     how='inner')\n",
        "\n",
        "# Drop the redundant 'Country_ISO' column after merging\n",
        "merged_df.drop(columns=['Country_ISO'], inplace=True)\n",

        "    if merged_df[col].isnull().any():\n",

      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "06801937"
      },
      "source": [
        "### Key Generalizations and Analysis:\n",
        "\n",
        "*   **Range of Years**: We have data spanning a significant period, allowing for trend analysis.\n",
        "*   **Indicator Variety**: The climate change indicators cover various aspects, from temperature change to GHG emissions, offering a multi-faceted view of climate impact.\n",
        "*   **Correlation Potential**: Intuitively, we expect an inverse relationship between `Value` (renewable energy consumption) and `GHG emissions`, and possibly `Temperature change`. Countries investing more in renewable energy might show lower emission levels or slower temperature increases.\n",
        "*   **Country-Specific Patterns**: Different countries will likely exhibit diverse patterns in both renewable energy adoption and climate change impacts, which can be explored through grouping and aggregation.\n",
        "*   **Data Distribution**: The `describe()` output gives us a glimpse into the distribution of numerical features. Some may be skewed, suggesting the need for transformations or robust modeling techniques.\n",
        "\n",
        "Now, let's move on to visualizing these patterns and relationships using Seaborn."
      ]
    },

        "## 6. Feature Scaling and Categorical Encoding\n",
        "\n",
        "To prepare our `merged_df` for machine learning algorithms, we need to perform two key preprocessing steps:\n",
        "\n",
        "1.  **Scaling Numerical Columns**: Numerical features often have different scales and ranges, which can affect the performance of many machine learning algorithms. `StandardScaler` will transform the data to have a mean of 0 and a standard deviation of 1.\n",
        "2.  **Encoding Categorical Columns**: Machine learning models typically require numerical input. Categorical features like 'Country' need to be converted into a numerical representation. `pd.get_dummies` is suitable for this, creating binary columns for each category."
      ]
    },

        "numerical_cols = merged_df.select_dtypes(include=np.number).columns.tolist()\n",

        "\n",
        "# Remove 'Year' from numerical columns if it's used as a direct feature, as it's often treated differently or for time series\n",
        "# For this task, we will scale 'Year' as well, assuming it contributes linearly to the models.\n",
        "\n",
        "print(f\"Numerical columns: {numerical_cols}\")\n",
        "print(f\"Categorical columns: {categorical_cols}\")"

      "source": [
        "### Insights on Scaling and Encoding:\n",
        "\n",
        "*   **Why Scaling?**: Numerical features like 'Value' (renewable energy consumption) and 'Temperature change' had very different scales. Scaling ensures that no single feature dominates the learning process, which is crucial for algorithms sensitive to feature magnitudes (e.g., linear regression, SVMs, neural networks).\n",
        "*   **Why Encoding?**: The 'Country' column, being a nominal categorical variable, needed to be converted into a numerical format. One-hot encoding creates a new binary column for each unique country (minus one to avoid multicollinearity), allowing the models to process this information effectively without assuming any ordinal relationship between countries.\n",
        "*   **Impact on Dataset**: The dataset's width (number of columns) has significantly increased due to one-hot encoding, as each unique country now has its own column. This is a common consequence of handling high-cardinality categorical features.\n",
        "\n",
        "Now our data is fully preprocessed and ready for splitting into training and testing sets, and then applying machine learning algorithms."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "af225aa4"
      },
      "source": [
        "## 7. Splitting Data into Training and Testing Sets\n",
        "\n",
        "Before applying machine learning algorithms, we need to split our preprocessed data into features (X) and target variables (y), and then further divide them into training and testing sets. We will define two different target variables:\n",
        "\n",
        "1.  **Regression Target (`y_reg`)**: `Value` (Scaled Renewable Energy Consumption).\n",
        "2.  **Classification Target (`y_clf`)**: A new binary column, `High_Renewable_Energy`, indicating whether the 'Value' is above the overall median, transformed to be 0 or 1."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "code",
      "metadata": {
        "colab": {
          "base_uri": "https://localhost:8080/"
        },
        "id": "3e6d9a2a",
        "outputId": "ef3ffe50-f33b-4a1f-d911-4eadd9e939c5"
      },
      "source": [
        "# Create the classification target variable 'High_Renewable_Energy'\n",
        "# We use the median of the *scaled* 'Value' column to create the binary target for consistency with the scaled feature 'Value'.\n",
        "median_scaled_value = merged_df['Value'].median()\n",
        "merged_df['High_Renewable_Energy'] = (merged_df['Value'] > median_scaled_value).astype(int)\n",
        "\n",
        "# Define features (X) and target variables (y)\n",
        "X = merged_df.drop(columns=['Value', 'High_Renewable_Energy'])\n",
        "y_reg = merged_df['Value']  # Target for regression\n",
        "y_clf = merged_df['High_Renewable_Energy']  # Target for classification\n",
        "\n",
        "print(\"Shape of X:\", X.shape)\n",
        "print(\"Shape of y_reg:\", y_reg.shape)\n",
        "print(\"Shape of y_clf:\", y_clf.shape)\n",
        "print(\"\\nHigh_Renewable_Energy distribution:\\n\", y_clf.value_counts())"
      ],
      "execution_count": null,
      "outputs": [
        {
          "output_type": "stream",
          "name": "stdout",
          "text": [
            "Shape of X: (14740, 135)\n",
            "Shape of y_reg: (14740,)\n",
            "Shape of y_clf: (14740,)\n",
            "\n",
            "High_Renewable_Energy distribution:\n",
            " High_Renewable_Energy\n",
            "0    9090\n",
            "1    5650\n",
            "Name: count, dtype: int64\n"
          ]
        }
      ]

      "cell_type": "code",
      "metadata": {
        "colab": {
          "base_uri": "https://localhost:8080/"

        "id": "a031ce9a",
        "outputId": "8a174883-e87b-444d-e9d7-aa4cb7feb2ab"
      },
      "source": [
        "# Split the data for regression\n",
        "X_train_reg, X_test_reg, y_train_reg, y_test_reg = train_test_split(X, y_reg, test_size=0.2, random_state=42)\n",
        "\n",
        "# Split the data for classification\n",
        "X_train_clf, X_test_clf, y_train_clf, y_test_clf = train_test_split(X, y_clf, test_size=0.2, random_state=42, stratify=y_clf)\n",
        "\n",
        "print(\"\\n--- Data Split for Regression ---\")\n",
        "print(f\"X_train_reg shape: {X_train_reg.shape}\")\n",

        "print(f\"X_train_clf shape: {X_train_clf.shape}\")\n",

        "print(f\"y_train_clf shape: {y_train_clf.shape}\")\n",
        "print(f\"y_test_clf shape: {y_test_clf.shape}\")"

      "outputs": [
        {

            "--- Data Split for Regression ---\n",

            "X_test_reg shape: (2948, 135)\n",
            "y_train_reg shape: (11792,)\n",
            "y_test_reg shape: (2948,)\n",
            "\n",
            "--- Data Split for Classification ---\n",
            "X_train_clf shape: (11792, 135)\n",
            "X_test_clf shape: (2948, 135)\n",

            "y_test_clf shape: (2948,)\n"
          ]
        }
      ]

    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "6f88001a"
      },
      "source": [
        "## 8. Applying Regression and Classification Algorithms\n",
        "\n",
        "Now that our data is split, we can apply the chosen algorithms:\n",
        "\n",
        "*   **Regression**: Linear Regression to predict `Value` (scaled renewable energy consumption).\n",
        "*   **Classification**: Logistic Regression to predict `High_Renewable_Energy` (whether renewable energy consumption is above median)."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "07ce1bd3"
      },
      "source": [
        "### Linear Regression Model"
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "code",
      "metadata": {
        "colab": {
          "base_uri": "https://localhost:8080/"
        },
        "id": "6091c3eb",
        "outputId": "4b1e3640-7acf-4e1f-f287-d08833a9b1ec"
      },
      "source": [
        "# Initialize and train the Linear Regression model\n",
        "linear_reg_model = LinearRegression()\n",
        "linear_reg_model.fit(X_train_reg, y_train_reg)\n",
        "\n",
        "# Make predictions on the test set\n",

      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "153727e6"
      },
      "source": [
        "## Final Insights and Model Error Explanations\n",
        "\n",
        "### Linear Regression Insights:\n",
        "\n",
        "*   **R-squared**: The R-squared value indicates how well the independent variables (Year, Temperature Change, and Country dummies) explain the variance in renewable energy consumption. A high R-squared (e.g., above 0.7-0.8) suggests that the model explains a large portion of the variability, while a low value indicates that many factors influencing renewable energy consumption are not captured by the model.\n",
        "*   **Mean Squared Error (MSE)**: MSE is a measure of the average of the squares of the errors. A lower MSE indicates that the model's predictions are closer to the actual values. The context of the scaled 'Value' is important here; a small MSE on scaled data suggests good performance relative to the scaled range.\n",
        "*   **Why the results are as they are**: If R-squared is high, it implies that factors like country-specific policies (captured by country dummies), general trends over time, and climate change indicators are good predictors of renewable energy consumption. If R-squared is low, it suggests that other socio-economic factors, technological advancements, or policy details not included in the dataset are also significant.\n",
        "\n",
        "### Logistic Regression Insights:\n",
        "\n",
        "*   **Accuracy**: The overall proportion of correctly predicted instances. While a good starting point, accuracy can be misleading in imbalanced datasets.\n",
        "*   **Precision**: For the 'High Renewable Energy' class, precision tells us how many of the instances predicted as 'High' were actually 'High'. High precision means fewer false positives.\n",
        "*   **Recall**: For the 'High Renewable Energy' class, recall tells us how many of the actual 'High' instances were correctly identified by the model. High recall means fewer false negatives.\n",
        "*   **F1-Score**: The harmonic mean of precision and recall, providing a balanced measure, especially useful when class distribution is uneven.\n",
        "*   **Confusion Matrix**: This matrix provides a breakdown of correct and incorrect predictions for each class. It helps identify if the model struggles more with one class (e.g., misclassifying 'Low' as 'High' or vice versa).\n",
        "*   **Why the results are as they are**: The performance of the Logistic Regression model in classifying 'High_Renewable_Energy' depends on how clearly renewable energy consumption can be separated into two classes based on the features. If the features provide distinct patterns for high vs. low consumption, the model will perform well. If there's a lot of overlap or noise, the performance metrics will be lower. For example, if many countries fluctuate around the median, it becomes harder for the model to make a clear distinction, leading to more misclassifications."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "0176a276"
      },
      "source": [
        "## 7. Splitting Data into Training and Testing Sets\n",
        "\n",
        "Before applying machine learning algorithms, we need to split our preprocessed data into features (X) and target variables (y), and then further divide them into training and testing sets. We will define two different target variables:\n",
        "\n",
        "1.  **Regression Target (`y_reg`)**: `Value` (Renewable Energy Consumption).\n",
        "2.  **Classification Target (`y_clf`)**: A new binary column, `High_Renewable_Energy`, indicating whether the 'Value' is above the overall median, transformed to be 0 or 1."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "code",
      "metadata": {
        "colab": {
          "base_uri": "https://localhost:8080/"
        },
        "id": "35fe69da",
        "outputId": "5fa69457-c1d2-43f0-e0ef-eb0f80e5bba8"
      },
      "source": [
        "# Create the classification target variable 'High_Renewable_Energy'\n",
        "# We use the unscaled 'Value' median for a more intuitive threshold\n",
        "# Let's get the median from the original renewable_energy_df to avoid issues with scaled values\n",
        "original_value_median = pd.read_csv('/content/renewable_energy.csv')['Value'].median()\n",
        "\n",
        "# Note: If we use the scaled 'Value' median, it will be close to 0\n",
        "# For better interpretability, let's derive it from the unscaled data context\n",
        "# However, since merged_df['Value'] is already scaled, we must be careful.\n",
        "# Let's re-calculate it based on the scaled 'Value' for consistency with the scaled feature 'Value'.\n",
        "# Median of a scaled variable is generally 0 if the original median is transformed.\n",
        "\n",
        "# For the purpose of creating a classification target after scaling:\n",
        "# If a value is above the median of the *scaled* 'Value' column, it's 'High_Renewable_Energy'\n",
        "median_scaled_value = merged_df['Value'].median()\n",
        "merged_df['High_Renewable_Energy'] = (merged_df['Value'] > median_scaled_value).astype(int)\n",
        "\n",
        "# Define features (X) and target variables (y)\n",
        "X = merged_df.drop(columns=['Value', 'High_Renewable_Energy'])\n",
        "y_reg = merged_df['Value']  # Target for regression\n",
        "y_clf = merged_df['High_Renewable_Energy']  # Target for classification\n",
        "\n",
        "print(\"Shape of X:\", X.shape)\n",
        "print(\"Shape of y_reg:\", y_reg.shape)\n",
        "print(\"Shape of y_clf:\", y_clf.shape)\n",
        "print(\"\\nHigh_Renewable_Energy distribution:\\n\", y_clf.value_counts())"
      ],
      "execution_count": null,
      "outputs": [
        {
          "output_type": "stream",
          "name": "stdout",
          "text": [
            "Shape of X: (14740, 135)\n",
            "Shape of y_reg: (14740,)\n",
            "Shape of y_clf: (14740,)\n",
            "\n",
            "High_Renewable_Energy distribution:\n",
            " High_Renewable_Energy\n",
            "0    9090\n",
            "1    5650\n",
            "Name: count, dtype: int64\n"
          ]
        }
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "code",
      "metadata": {
        "colab": {
          "base_uri": "https://localhost:8080/"
        },
        "id": "dc9c94b2",
        "outputId": "cc7084aa-6efe-4e22-9216-36d3ffba7fcb"
      },
      "source": [
        "# Split the data for regression\n",
        "X_train_reg, X_test_reg, y_train_reg, y_test_reg = train_test_split(X, y_reg, test_size=0.2, random_state=42)\n",
        "\n",
        "# Split the data for classification\n",
        "X_train_clf, X_test_clf, y_train_clf, y_test_clf = train_test_split(X, y_clf, test_size=0.2, random_state=42, stratify=y_clf)\n",
        "\n",
        "print(\"\\n--- Data Split for Regression ---\")\n",
        "print(f\"X_train_reg shape: {X_train_reg.shape}\")\n",

        "print(\"\\n--- Data Split for Classification ---\")\n",
        "print(f\"X_train_clf shape: {X_train_clf.shape}\")\n",
        "print(f\"X_test_clf shape: {X_test_clf.shape}\")\n",
        "print(f\"y_train_clf shape: {y_train_clf.shape}\")\n",
        "print(f\"y_test_clf shape: {y_test_clf.shape}\")"
      ],
      "execution_count": null,
      "outputs": [

          "output_type": "stream",

The context of the scaled 'Value' is important here; a small MSE on scaled data suggests good performance relative to the scaled range.\n",
        "*   **Why the results are as they are**: If R-squared is high, it implies that factors like country-specific policies (captured by country dummies), general trends over time, and climate change indicators are good predictors of renewable energy consumption. If R-squared is low, it suggests that other socio-economic factors, technological advancements, or policy details not included in the dataset are also significant.\n",
        "\n",
        "### Logistic Regression Insights:\n",
        "\n",
        "*   **Accuracy**: The overall proportion of correctly predicted instances. While a good starting point, accuracy can be misleading in imbalanced datasets.\n",
        "*   **Precision**: For the 'High Renewable Energy' class, precision tells us how many of the instances predicted as 'High' were actually 'High'. High precision means fewer false positives.\n",
        "*   **Recall**: For the 'High Renewable Energy' class, recall tells us how many of the actual 'High' instances were correctly identified by the model. High recall means fewer false negatives.\n",
        "*   **F1-Score**: The harmonic mean of precision and recall, providing a balanced measure, especially useful when class distribution is uneven.\n",
        "*   **Confusion Matrix**: This matrix provides a breakdown of correct and incorrect predictions for each class. It helps identify if the model struggles more with one class (e.g., misclassifying 'Low' as 'High' or vice versa).\n",
        "*   **Why the results are as they are**: The performance of the Logistic Regression model in classifying 'High_Renewable_Energy' depends on how clearly renewable energy consumption can be separated into two classes based on the features. If the features provide distinct patterns for high vs. low consumption, the model will perform well. If there's a lot of overlap or noise, the performance metrics will be lower. For example, if many countries fluctuate around the median, it becomes harder for the model to make a clear distinction, leading to more misclassifications."
      ]
    }
  ]
